  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_8747cea8-3bda-42e8-91c3-0819401c1b0a'
      });

<div id="descriptionContainer">
  
      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer13003320562146909039">Believing William Legrand to have gone insane following an insect bite, his friend initially decries his quest for gold as the ramblings of a madman. Yet when Legrand's conviction fails to waiver, they set off on a bizarre journey, accompanied by Jupiter, Legrand's loyal and equally skeptical servant. What follows is a strange tale of coded messages, hidden treasure, and u</span>
  <span id="freeText13003320562146909039" style="display:none">Believing William Legrand to have gone insane following an insect bite, his friend initially decries his quest for gold as the ramblings of a madman. Yet when Legrand's conviction fails to waiver, they set off on a bizarre journey, accompanied by Jupiter, Legrand's loyal and equally skeptical servant. What follows is a strange tale of coded messages, hidden treasure, and uncanny prophecy that will both enthrall and baffle even the most perceptive readers.Part horror story, part detective fiction, <u>The Gold Bug</u> is an ingenious tale bearing all the hallmarks of Poe's extraordinary narrative skill. It is presented here with <u>The Sphinx</u>, a similarly themed and equally disturbing short story. Wonderfully versatile as an author and best known for his tales of terror and the macabre, Edgar Allan Poe (1809–1849) holds a venerable place in the history of American literature.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13003320562146909039" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_1354955782" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1354955782" />
    <a title="Tadiana ✩Night Owl☽" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/8734459-tadiana-night-owl"><img alt="Tadiana ✩Night Owl☽" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1603085284p2/8734459.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1354955782?book_show_action=true">Aug 04, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tadiana ✩Night Owl☽" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tadiana ✩Night Owl☽" href="/user/show/8734459-tadiana-night-owl">Tadiana ✩Night Owl☽</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8734459-tadiana-night-owl?shelf=mystery">mystery</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8734459-tadiana-night-owl?shelf=dated-social-attitudes">dated-social-attitudes</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8734459-tadiana-night-owl?shelf=the-shorts">the-shorts</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8734459-tadiana-night-owl?shelf=gutenberg-freebie">gutenberg-freebie</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8734459-tadiana-night-owl?shelf=avast-ye-scurvy-dogs">avast-ye-scurvy-dogs</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1354955782" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13561470428390285785"><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1438758338i/15750046._SX540_.jpg' width='341' height='291' alt='description' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/> <br/><br/>"The Gold-Bug," written in 1843, was one of Edgar Allen Poe's most popular stories. He actually won a $100 prize for it in a newspaper's writing contest, probably the most he was ever paid for any of his works. It tells the story of William Legrand, who finds and deciphers an old parchment with a hidden message that leads to a pirate's buried treasure -- thus taking advantage of the 19th century public's enthusiasm for both cryptography and buried treasure.<br/><br/>The cipher on the parchment is a relat</span>
  <span id="freeText13561470428390285785" style="display:none"><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1438758338i/15750046._SX540_.jpg" width="341" height="291" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"> <br><br>"The Gold-Bug," written in 1843, was one of Edgar Allen Poe's most popular stories. He actually won a $100 prize for it in a newspaper's writing contest, probably the most he was ever paid for any of his works. It tells the story of William Legrand, who finds and deciphers an old parchment with a hidden message that leads to a pirate's buried treasure -- thus taking advantage of the 19th century public's enthusiasm for both cryptography and buried treasure.<br><br>The cipher on the parchment is a relatively simple substitution cipher, but the story of following the clues and finding the treasure is intriguing and creative, especially for the time it was written.<br><br>My big problem with this story is Legrand's servant, a stereotypical black character, Jupiter, a former slave who has insisted on remaining with the family that previously owned him. He's used for comic effect in the story, speaking with a broad dialect that's full of "massa" talk and foolish superstitions. It's seriously wince-inducing reading. But if you can get past that, keeping in mind that this was written about 170 years ago, pre-Civil War, the rest of the story is a fun adventure. <br><br>Available to read online many places, including<br><a href="http://xroads.virginia.edu/~hyper/POE/gold_bug.html" rel="nofollow noopener">here</a>, or you can download it for free at Gutenberg.org.<br><br><i>Art credit: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://jackodeco.deviantart.com/art/The-gold-bug-Edgar-Poe-1-426774341">http://jackodeco.deviantart.com/art/T...</a></i></span>
  <a data-text-id="13561470428390285785" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer973879534" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4802467744378571595"><i>The Gold Bug</i> tells the adventure of Mr. William Legrand, a recluse and entomologist, who finds a gold bug which eventually leads him to a buried treasure of pirates. <br/><br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1403456981i/10095622._SX540_.jpg' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><blockquote>
  <i>He had hunted down and secured, with Jupiter's assistance, a scarabaeus which he believed to be totally new, but in respect to which he wished to have my opinion on the morrow.<br/>"And why not tonight?" I asked, rubbing my hands over the blaze, and wishing the whole tribe of scarabaei at the devil.</i>
</blockquote><br/>The narrator is very skeptical, and</span>
  <span id="freeText4802467744378571595" style="display:none"><i>The Gold Bug</i> tells the adventure of Mr. William Legrand, a recluse and entomologist, who finds a gold bug which eventually leads him to a buried treasure of pirates. <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1403456981i/10095622._SX540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><blockquote>
  <i>He had hunted down and secured, with Jupiter's assistance, a scarabaeus which he believed to be totally new, but in respect to which he wished to have my opinion on the morrow.<br>"And why not tonight?" I asked, rubbing my hands over the blaze, and wishing the whole tribe of scarabaei at the devil.</i>
</blockquote><br>The narrator is very skeptical, and doesn’t believe in Legrand’s phantasmagoria. But worrying for him, he decides to help him in his quest and tries to save him from his own “madness”.<br> <br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1403456981i/10095623.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>The blurb says it is a disturbing, partly horror tale: well, neither horror nor disturbing parts here. This story is about a treasure hunt and Legrand’s way of succeeding: a series of coincidences and deductions, deciphering clues and cryptanalysis. <br><br><b>With a great storyline and amusing narration, it is a highly entertaining story.</b> <br><br></span>
  <a data-text-id="4802467744378571595" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9465536032406014063"><b>3.5 out of 5 stars</b><br/><br/>This is a story about a treasure hunt that starts with finding a gold beetle, and proceeds from there with adventure and ciphers and ingenious explanations.<br/><br/>The narrator is the usual nameless/faceless one that appears in most of Poe's stories. This time, though, he's not unreliable.<br/><br/>Truly recommended for fans of Poe and/or short stories.</span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1064381143" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9975948020405422830">The first time I read this, I thought it was very boring. Having just read it again, in my epic journey to read every single goddamned thing that Poe has ever written - (I shall be married with kids by the time I am finished; or, more likely, rotting beneath an unmarked grave as all my friends go on without realizing I'm even dead) - I guess I enjoyed the story a little bit more. But hell, by the time I finished it, I think I basically had the same impressions. I give it for credit for making se</span>
  <span id="freeText9975948020405422830" style="display:none">The first time I read this, I thought it was very boring. Having just read it again, in my epic journey to read every single goddamned thing that Poe has ever written - (I shall be married with kids by the time I am finished; or, more likely, rotting beneath an unmarked grave as all my friends go on without realizing I'm even dead) - I guess I enjoyed the story a little bit more. But hell, by the time I finished it, I think I basically had the same impressions. I give it for credit for making sense (excluding, of course, that fucking ridiculous dialogue of Jupiter the Negro) which is, so far, something to be cherished with Poe's stories. (My God ... have you read <i>Von Kempelen And His Discovery</i>? What the fuck was <i>that</i> about?)<br />I also didn't like it how the mystery is solved and then, after you get the answers and all, the guy still takes painstaking detail to explain to you how he accomplished it, even though it's pretty damned obvious. Overall it wasn't a bad story. I just prefer the depraved and morbid stuff that this supposed genius can writes when he so chooses. </span>
  <a data-text-id="9975948020405422830" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">

<span id="freeTextContainer10354263047138139506">Finally, humour from Poe! Sadly, most of it comes at the expense of a negro servant.<br/><br/>Otherwise, it's an adventure story involving a cypher. I remember the days when emails sometimes came as gibberish because of some bug (not a gold-bug, though) and one could decypher them using more or less the same methods (knowing the email probably began with 'Hello' or words to that meaning helped a lot, of course). Anyway, Poe's story was a huge inspiration for many others - writers, as well as, they say, th</span>
  <span id="freeText10354263047138139506" style="display:none">Finally, humour from Poe! Sadly, most of it comes at the expense of a negro servant.<br /><br />Otherwise, it's an adventure story involving a cypher. I remember the days when emails sometimes came as gibberish because of some bug (not a gold-bug, though) and one could decypher them using more or less the same methods (knowing the email probably began with 'Hello' or words to that meaning helped a lot, of course). Anyway, Poe's story was a huge inspiration for many others - writers, as well as, they say, the great cryptographer William Friedman - and it's short and entertaining, so, worth reading.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10354263047138139506" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

I don't really find the idea very plausible, since I would think it would be obvious that to look at the "monster" would require a different focus than looking at the scenery. I could believe a momentary illusion, but not something that would stand up to scrutiny.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer12099003540868317927">If you like tales of adventure that involve treasure maps, ciphers, pirate gold and buried treasure than this is a story for you. An excellent read for both young and old.<br/><br/>In the story Poe has the narrator describe Legrand as thus; "I found him well educated, with unusual powers of mind, but infected with misanthropy, and subject to perverse moods of alternate enthusiasm and melancholy" (p. 2). If anyone would know about those perverse moods it would be Poe. Dr. Kay R. Jamison speaking at a John </span>
  <span id="freeText12099003540868317927" style="display:none">If you like tales of adventure that involve treasure maps, ciphers, pirate gold and buried treasure than this is a story for you. An excellent read for both young and old.<br /><br />In the story Poe has the narrator describe Legrand as thus; "I found him well educated, with unusual powers of mind, but infected with misanthropy, and subject to perverse moods of alternate enthusiasm and melancholy" (p. 2). If anyone would know about those perverse moods it would be Poe. Dr. Kay R. Jamison speaking at a John Hopkins symposium on mood disorders, argued that Poe is probably one of many writers and artists who have suffered from the ailment, which is marked by wild swings between frenzied, compulsive activity and crippling despair. The seasonal pattern of his moods is typical of manic-depression, she said. He was often manic in January and February, and depressed in the late summer and fall. And Poe's moods became more pronounced as he grew older, another sign of the disease. . . . But, she added, the writer thoroughly mined his dark moods for literary material. A cheerful Poe, she said, might never have gone on to write some of his most widely read works -- "Tha Raven," perhaps, or "The Pit and the Pendulum" or "Annabel Lee." <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.baltimoresun.com/news/bs-xpm-1993-04-28-1993118157-story.html">https://www.baltimoresun.com/news/bs-...</a><br /><b>
  <u>Interesting Tidbit</u>
</b> Alfred Butts the inventor of Scrabble, the popular board game was inspired by the Cipher used in Edgar Allan Poe’s treasure hunt story, <i>The Gold Bug.</i></span>
  <a data-text-id="12099003540868317927" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

I'm familiar with both Charleston and Sullivan's Island, so it made the story even more interesting. </span>

<div id="review_1036925002" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1036925002" />
    <a title="Isaac Cooper" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/25791416-isaac-cooper"><img alt="Isaac Cooper" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1393146442p2/25791416.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1036925002?book_show_action=true">Aug 26, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Isaac Cooper" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Isaac Cooper" href="/user/show/25791416-isaac-cooper">Isaac Cooper</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1036925002" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7358372615744993622">Well, I read this to the end, so that’s something. This is my first Poe and, surprise, it wasn’t terrible! The Gold Bug is a short novella/story, quite short indeed, about a treasure hunt, I suppose you’d say? But there isn’t much treasure hunting, really, but it’s the main focus of the story, the treasure. I enjoyed the first three quarters of The Gold Bug, seeing the narrator and his eccentric friend (and his ‘negro,’ Jupiter, who, by the way, has some of the worst ‘black writing’ I’ve ever se</span>
  <span id="freeText7358372615744993622" style="display:none">Well, I read this to the end, so that’s something. This is my first Poe and, surprise, it wasn’t terrible! The Gold Bug is a short novella/story, quite short indeed, about a treasure hunt, I suppose you’d say? But there isn’t much treasure hunting, really, but it’s the main focus of the story, the treasure. I enjoyed the first three quarters of The Gold Bug, seeing the narrator and his eccentric friend (and his ‘negro,’ Jupiter, who, by the way, has some of the worst ‘black writing’ I’ve ever seen, worse than Infinite Jest … okay, maybe not that bad) on the quest for treasure. The last part of the story, the final act if you will, was a huge let down, though. It’s just Legrand, the eccentric friend, giving an unimaginably long expository speech about how he managed to decrypt and find the location of the treasure. <br /><br />This was a big letdown for me, because I was much more interested in what exactly the three men would do with the treasure, how they would split it up, whether there’d be a dispute or some jealously over who gets what and so on, but nothing like that happened. The Gold Bug just ends after Legrand’s lengthy explanation of how he found the treasure, it just ends, like that! I didn’t mind it, though. The Gold Bug gets the dubious honour of being something I’ve actually read all the way through in the last few months. I suppose it helped that it was so short, but the writing was also mostly solid. The Gold Bug makes me want to read more Poe. <br /><br />Also, I like how Poe just wrote, like that’s all he did. I remember reading that Poe was one of the few more modern writers who just … wrote, and wrote, and wrote. I always respect writers (and people in other professions) like this, who just focus on one thing their whole lives, dedicate themselves to it. So, would I recommend this? <br /><br />Hmm, yeah, it gets a recommendation. It’s not the greatest book I’ve ever read, but I found it relatively enjoyable. I wish there was an explanation as to what each of them did with the treasure after that, a better final act but, hey, The Gold Bug didn’t offend me, make me angry, like Quite Ugly One Morning did, and that’s always a good thing.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="7358372615744993622" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_241780934" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/241780934" />
    <a title="Pam" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3220056-pam"><img alt="Pam" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1541422058p2/3220056.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/241780934?book_show_action=true">Nov 30, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Pam" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Pam" href="/user/show/3220056-pam">Pam</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Nook 2940012642271" href="/book/show/10911050-the-gold-bug">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3220056-pam?shelf=classics">classics</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer241780934" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13143359780407478693">I first read <i>The Gold Bug</i> several years ago, when I was only about ten years old, maybe even younger. I remember making -- or more accurately, <i>trying</i> to make -- my own treasure map and riddle after I finished it. At that time, I didn't know who Edgar Allan Poe was, but I decided after reading this story that he is a brilliant writer. I can see now that <i>The Tell-Tale Heart</i> is better written, words- and tone-wise, but <i>The Gold Bug</i> was what made me like Poe as a writer. No doubt, <i>The Gold Bug</i> stren</span>
  <span id="freeText13143359780407478693" style="display:none">I first read <i>The Gold Bug</i> several years ago, when I was only about ten years old, maybe even younger. I remember making -- or more accurately, <i>trying</i> to make -- my own treasure map and riddle after I finished it. At that time, I didn't know who Edgar Allan Poe was, but I decided after reading this story that he is a brilliant writer. I can see now that <i>The Tell-Tale Heart</i> is better written, words- and tone-wise, but <i>The Gold Bug</i> was what made me like Poe as a writer. No doubt, <i>The Gold Bug</i> strengthened my interest in riddles, and started my interest in ciphers.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13143359780407478693" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('2zxAVM8hgp1Sa4BsOg5RuAtXX7EwpxGL4WUNtyD/1cEjGZoB2VRo7efWJrxtxNwvzo396i/V+kQLFZbtuIldBw==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('sPuFdIVvqGEBKdlaLTSlInbxN9oQjennasdkMjVhOBBI3l8hkxpCEbSUf4p6/ii1syuVgQ//AiiAt/9orRew1g==')})
  }

Oscar Fingal O\'Flahertie Wills Wilde (1854–1900) was an Irish author, playwright and poet. After writing in different forms throughout the 1880s, he became one of London\'s most popular playwrights in the ea<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText1854746808897664234\" style=\"display:none\">The Birthday of the Infanta by Oscar Wilde. Oscar Fingal O\'Flahertie Wills Wilde (1854–1900) was an Irish author, playwright and poet. After writing in different forms throughout the 1880s, he became one of London\'s most popular playwrights in the early 1890s. Today he is remembered for his epigrams, his novel The Picture of Dorian Gray, his plays, as well as the circumstances of his imprisonment and early death.&quot;The Birthday of the Infanta&quot; is a historical fiction short story for children by the Irish author Oscar Wilde. It was first published in the 1891 anthology House of Pomegranates, which also includes &quot;The Young King\', &quot;The Fisherman and his Soul&quot; and &quot;The Star-Child&quot;.The action of &quot;The Birthday of the Infanta&quot; takes place in Spain at an unspecified point in the past. It is the twelfth birthday of the Infanta, the only daughter, and only child, of the King of Spain. For her entertainment, an ugly young dwarf dancer is brought to the court. The Dwarf is completely unaware of how hideous he looks and does not realize that the reason that others laugh in his presence is because they are mocking his appearance. Reginald Dwayne Betts confronts the funk of postincarceration existence and examines prison not as a static space, but as a force that enacts pressure throughout a person’s life.\n\nThe poems move between traditional and newfound forms with power and agility—from revolutionary found poems created by redacting court documents to the astonishing crown of sonnets that serves as the volume’s radiant conclusion. Drawing inspiration from lawsuits filed on behalf of the incarcerated, the redaction poems focus on the ways we exploit and erase the poor and imprisoned from public consciousness. Traditionally, redaction erases what is top secret; in Felon, Betts redacts what is superfluous, bringing into focus the profound failures of the criminal justice system and the inadequacy of the <span id="freeTextContainer10936572335263842419">The name Poe brings to mind images of murderers and madmen, premature burials, and mysterious women who return from the dead. His works have been in print since 1827 and include such literary classics as <i>The Tell-Tale Heart</i>, <i>The Raven</i>, and The Fall of the House of Usher. This versatile writer’s oeuvre includes short stories, poetry, a novel, a textbook, a book of scientific theory, and hundreds of</span>
  <span id="freeText10936572335263842419" style="display:none">The name Poe brings to mind images of murderers and madmen, premature burials, and mysterious women who return from the dead. His works have been in print since 1827 and include such literary classics as <i>The Tell-Tale Heart</i>, <i>The Raven</i>, and The Fall of the House of Usher. This versatile writer’s oeuvre includes short stories, poetry, a novel, a textbook, a book of scientific theory, and hundreds of essays and book reviews. He is widely acknowledged as the inventor of the modern detective story and an innovator in the science fiction genre, but he made his living as America’s first great literary critic and theoretician. Poe’s reputation today rests primarily on his tales of terror as well as on his haunting lyric poetry.<br /><br />Just as the bizarre characters in Poe’s stories have captured the public imagination so too has Poe himself. He is seen as a morbid, mysterious figure lurking in the shadows of moonlit cemeteries or crumbling castles. This is the Poe of legend. But much of what we know about Poe is wrong, the product of a biography written by one of his enemies in an attempt to defame the author’s name.<br /><br />The real Poe was born to traveling actors in Boston on January 19, 1809. Edgar was the second of three children. His other brother William Henry Leonard Poe would also become a poet before his early death, and Poe’s sister Rosalie Poe would grow up to teach penmanship at a Richmond girls’ school. Within three years of Poe’s birth both of his parents had died, and he was taken in by the wealthy tobacco merchant John Allan and his wife Frances Valentine Allan in Richmond, Virginia while Poe’s siblings went to live with other families. Mr. Allan would rear Poe to be a businessman and a Virginia gentleman, but Poe had dreams of being a writer in emulation of his childhood hero the British poet Lord Byron. Early poetic verses found written in a young Poe’s handwriting on the backs of Allan’s ledger sheets reveal how little interest Poe had in the tobacco business.<br /><br />For more information, please see <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Edgar_allan_poe">http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Edgar_al...</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="10936572335263842419" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
</div>
